<div id="review_213965600" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/213965600" />
    <a title="Ryan Groesbeck" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2376590-ryan-groesbeck"><img alt="Ryan Groesbeck" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1249357302p2/2376590.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/213965600?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan Groesbeck" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan Groesbeck" href="/user/show/2376590-ryan-groesbeck">Ryan Groesbeck</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2376590-ryan-groesbeck?shelf=dorothy-dunnett">dorothy-dunnett</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer213965600" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4736815498801226605">I was kind of disappointed in this one...not because it wasn't good, but because it's not as good as Dunnet has led me to expect out of her work. The scenery was fantastically drawn, as usual, and the plotting seemed tight. <br/><br/>I think it suffered from a lack of a major villain, but perhaps that's just coming down off the high of the brilliant evilness of Graham Malett in the middle entries on this series. The ending was odd and despite the clear attempt at an "AHA" moment to explain everything, thi</span>
  <span id="freeText4736815498801226605" style="display:none">I was kind of disappointed in this one...not because it wasn't good, but because it's not as good as Dunnet has led me to expect out of her work. The scenery was fantastically drawn, as usual, and the plotting seemed tight. <br /><br />I think it suffered from a lack of a major villain, but perhaps that's just coming down off the high of the brilliant evilness of Graham Malett in the middle entries on this series. The ending was odd and despite the clear attempt at an "AHA" moment to explain everything, this one fell a little flat and I was still confused about exactly what had transpired after it had all been theoretically explained to me. <br /><br />It did excel in character development though -- Lymond was particularly well drawn in this installment, as choices he's made in previous entries continue to catch up with him. Either the blurb for this one or for the next one I think makes a comment about "increasingly successful professionally, increasingly isolated in his personal life" and that's an excellent sum-up. There are some good scenes with his temporary(?) wife Phillippa, and there are people as usual attempting to nefariously kill Lymond from the shadows, but it lacked the clean punch of earlier installments. <br /><br />Maybe a re-read would help make some of these various points clearer, but the ending and abrupt solution to some of his political woes in England seemed a trifle too contrived for me -- worst outing in the series so far. Which, again, is not to say that it's a bad outing...but in such stellar company, this one does not compare favorably. <br /><br />ETA (3/5/2013) : I've upgraded this from a 3 to a 5 -- I think I had wrong expectations of this the first time, and after two subsequent readthroughs I enjoyed it a lot more for an introspective character piece instead of shoehorning it into a Heroic Action Adventure. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4736815498801226605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1200504795" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1200504795" />
    <a title="Nicole" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10369370-nicole"><img alt="Nicole" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483152509p2/10369370.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1200504795?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole" href="/user/show/10369370-nicole">Nicole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10369370-nicole?shelf=a-n-book-club">a-n-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10369370-nicole?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10369370-nicole?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1200504795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13324784048386869328">Up until this one, I felt like each book in the Lymond series got better. This is the first one that I thought was a step back. I just kept losing interest, and it wasn't until her usual heart-pounding-one-twist-after-another final pages (damn, this woman can write a climax!) that it really measured up to the others.<br/><br/>Maybe it's that the Russia angle didn't interest me as much as the previous settings (Scotland, France, Malta, Turkey). It has its moments but is on the whole rather boring.<br/><br/>It's stre</span>
  <span id="freeText13324784048386869328" style="display:none">Up until this one, I felt like each book in the Lymond series got better. This is the first one that I thought was a step back. I just kept losing interest, and it wasn't until her usual heart-pounding-one-twist-after-another final pages (damn, this woman can write a climax!) that it really measured up to the others.<br /><br />Maybe it's that the Russia angle didn't interest me as much as the previous settings (Scotland, France, Malta, Turkey). It has its moments but is on the whole rather boring.<br /><br />It's strengths are classic Dunnett - characters and relationships. In that vein, a few particular highlights, first and foremost Phillipa; compelling as a child, we watched her come of age in the Turkish seraglio, and now here she is a full blown woman who can match - and even best - Lymond at his own games, not to mention the wiley women of the English Royal Court (Queen Elizabeth even makes an appearance, which is awesome.) Richard Chancellor is a new and ingenious construction: solid and noble and intrepid. A rare person who can teach Lymond something he doesn't already know (in this case, sailing and navigation), which is saying quite a lot. Lymond's men of St Mary's, new (Danny Hislop, Ludo D'Harcourt) and old (Blacklock, Hoddim, Guthrie, Plummer) continue to enrich the pages. And then there's Lymond himself, an onion with endless layers, who continues to grow and evolve and, even, strengthen.<br /><br />Relationships: the relationship between Lymond and Guzel is fascinating and beautiful. Their partnership is brilliant - they are the ultimate power couple. Lymond's relationship with his family is a depth worth sounding over and over again, and though the members thereof take up very little space on the page, both Richard and Sybilla make very powerful appearances at key moments. Brilliantly orchestrated. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13324784048386869328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_734904015" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/734904015" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1193329-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584278428p2/1193329.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/734904015?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/1193329-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1193329-stuart?shelf=white-authors">white-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1193329-stuart?shelf=non-american-authors">non-american-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1193329-stuart?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1193329-stuart?shelf=female-authors">female-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer734904015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3121044146276958783">After following Lymond as he overcomes limiting social orders, international intrigue, a hero as perfect an archetype as he, and his own weaknesses, the reader is presented with Lymond in a battle with himself.<br/><br/>The book follows Lymond on a journey to Russia, where he seeks greatness through the abnegation of his own humanity. Humanity being inseparable, it slowly claims him back, drawing him from Russia back to Scotland but also drawing him from the most dizzying of impersonal heights (as the sup</span>
  <span id="freeText3121044146276958783" style="display:none">After following Lymond as he overcomes limiting social orders, international intrigue, a hero as perfect an archetype as he, and his own weaknesses, the reader is presented with Lymond in a battle with himself.<br /><br />The book follows Lymond on a journey to Russia, where he seeks greatness through the abnegation of his own humanity. Humanity being inseparable, it slowly claims him back, drawing him from Russia back to Scotland but also drawing him from the most dizzying of impersonal heights (as the supreme commander of Russia's fledgling national army) through guarded friendships and tragedy all the way into levity, music and love. <br /><br />Deeply gratifying without providing fan service, rich with history and description, The Ringed Castle might very well be the pinnacle of the series.<br /><br />The series as a whole proves more and more intricate with the first and second book providing an arc wherein Lymond is at odds with society--first Scotland, then France.<br /><br />The second and third books trace the relationship between Lymond and the de Guise family, while the third and fourth detail an epic, sprawling battle between Lymond and an incredible nemesis. The fourth book depicts Lymond losing his humanity while the fifth shows him recovering it. To avoid spoilers I won't narrate what I believe the arc between the fifth and sixth books are, but I am sure it will prove to be rewarding and tense.<br /><br />I will certainly return to the series in the future, and especially this installment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3121044146276958783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_639529120" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/639529120" />
    <a title="Alexandra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20671980-alexandra"><img alt="Alexandra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369435300p2/20671980.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/639529120?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alexandra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alexandra" href="/user/show/20671980-alexandra">Alexandra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer639529120" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11431092755894082147">This particular book of the series explores Lymond's darker side; all through, one wonders, "is he changing for good?" "Is this the new Lymond?" The author continues to twist and turn every portion of the narrative, while giving us a taste of time, place, and national character which seems incredibly well-researched. It is impossible to render such a set of fascinating characters in six dense books, without having some major personality changes and reverses of fortune. This one, set largely in R</span>
  <span id="freeText11431092755894082147" style="display:none">This particular book of the series explores Lymond's darker side; all through, one wonders, "is he changing for good?" "Is this the new Lymond?" The author continues to twist and turn every portion of the narrative, while giving us a taste of time, place, and national character which seems incredibly well-researched. It is impossible to render such a set of fascinating characters in six dense books, without having some major personality changes and reverses of fortune. This one, set largely in Russia under Tsar Ivan, fascinates and educates. At the end, I could not wait to pick up Book Six.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11431092755894082147" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer872417938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11013591754829110016">The fifth book in the very excellent historical series of The Lymond Chronicles. What makes this book so compelling? Well the chinks are starting to appear in the near impregnable armour of Lymond. All is due to the very strong presence of his "wife in name only" Philippa. It's satisfying to see Philippa come into her own here and match Lymond word for word.<br/><br/>This is a wonderful book in it's own right. Worthy characters, a convoluted and intriguing plot. Perhaps more subdued than previous books, c</span>
  <span id="freeText11013591754829110016" style="display:none">The fifth book in the very excellent historical series of The Lymond Chronicles. What makes this book so compelling? Well the chinks are starting to appear in the near impregnable armour of Lymond. All is due to the very strong presence of his "wife in name only" Philippa. It's satisfying to see Philippa come into her own here and match Lymond word for word.<br /><br />This is a wonderful book in it's own right. Worthy characters, a convoluted and intriguing plot. Perhaps more subdued than previous books, certainly not as "swashbuckling" in action but well suited to the events portrayed within the pages. A keeper.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11013591754829110016" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17128406743451304882">Dorothy Dunnett's books have taken over my life at the moment. They are amazing and there really isn't anything quite like them. I also never thought I would be googling the significance of calling someone a heifer in Russian at 2am! <br/> She uses the book to tease the reader in a similar way to a cat playing with a mouse. <br/> This was in a way a bit of a linking book inbetween Pawns in Frankincense and Checkmate however Philippa and Lymond really develop as characters. Lymond tries to become like a ma</span>
  <span id="freeText17128406743451304882" style="display:none">Dorothy Dunnett's books have taken over my life at the moment. They are amazing and there really isn't anything quite like them. I also never thought I would be googling the significance of calling someone a heifer in Russian at 2am! <br /> She uses the book to tease the reader in a similar way to a cat playing with a mouse. <br /> This was in a way a bit of a linking book inbetween Pawns in Frankincense and Checkmate however Philippa and Lymond really develop as characters. Lymond tries to become like a machine but his feelings are more obvious as a result and Philippa just steals the second half of the book. I'm trying not to rush through reading them but it's too difficult not to. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17128406743451304882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qJn88v0X1S+AyVqrtrP0kshJnXyq8FFJrc7bx4mDzleD/L3CZmtAvycOhHUrHasTbQJAgiGA3YEnQQSHdy6Glw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zYVMYk5yISnQ6ESnfCYOvFBHB9bfDIDrrbtd3wAWVLXm4A1S1Q60uXcvmnnhiFE99QzaKFR8DCMnNIKf/rscdQ==')})
  }
//]]>

Richard Shelton first came to southeastern Arizona in the 1950s as a soldier stationed at Fort Huachuca. He soon fell in love with the region and upon his discharge found a job as a schoolteacher in nearby Bisbee. Now a university professor and respected poet living in Tucson, still in love with the Southwestern deserts, Shelton sets off for Bisbee on a not-uncommon day trip. Along the way, he reflects on the history of the area, on the beauty of the landscape, and on his own life. Couched within the narrative of his journey are passages revealing Shelton\'s deep familiarity with the region\'s natural and human history. Whether conveying the mystique of tarantulas or describing the mountain-studded topography, he brings a poet\'s eye to this seemingly desolate country. His observations on human habitation touch on Tombstone, &quot;the town too tough to die,&quot; on ghost towns that perhaps weren\'t as tough, and on Bisbee itself, a once prosperous mining town now an outpost for the arts and a destination for tourists. What he finds there is both a broad view of his past and a glimpse of that city\'s possible future.Going Back to Bisbee explores a part of America with which many readers may not be familiar. But before Stephanie Alexander penned a word for the emerging food media, let alone for The Cook\'s Companion, she had spent decades avidly documenting food experiences.\n\nShaped by her mother\'s dedication to good food and her father\'s love of reading, she trained as a librarian and all the while observed, assessed and re-created the dishes she loved. Her monthly university allowance rarely lasted more than a week – all spent on pan-fried flounder and chestnut Mont Blanc.\n\nShe was seduced over pain Poîlane while working as an au pair in Paris, and later over ackee and saltfish in London. In 1966, with no formal culinary training and a newborn baby, but brimming with confidence and sheer determination, she opened Jamaica House with her first husband. The personal toll was great and it was many years until she emerged on the restaurant scene again.\n\nStephanie\'s Restaurant would become part of Melbourne food folklore, permanently raising the bar for restaurant dining in Australia. At the time of its opening, in 1976, a salad to most people meant iceberg lettuce, no-one had heard of goat\'s cheese and ginger came in a tin. Over the next twenty-one years, in her quest for the sort of produce she had enjoyed while living and travelling in Europe, Stephanie championed small local suppliers or grew it herself.\n\nHer indefatigable determination and single-minded vision have influenced – and sometimes intimidated – a generation of chefs, cooks and diners. And now her Kitchen Garden Foundation is inspiring tens of thousands of primary school children across Australia to grow and cook their own food.\n\nA Cook\'s Life is a very personal account of one During this period, accelerated change saw Britain’s turbulent entry into the modern age, and clothing reflected these transformations. Starting with the intimate perspective of clothing the self, Dress in the Age of Jane Austen moves outward through the social and cultural spheres of home, village, countryside, and cities, and into the wider national and global realms, exploring the varied ways people dressed to inhabit these environments. Jane Austen’s famously observant fictional writings, as well as her letters, provide the entry point for examining the Regency age’s rich complexity of fashion, dress, and textiles for men and women in their contemporary contexts.   Lavishly illustrated with paintings, drawings, historic garments, and fashion plates—including many previously unpublished images—this authoritative yet accessible book will help readers visualize the external selves of Austen’s immortal characters as clearly as she wrote of their internal ones.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8361.Dorothy_Dunnett"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1554147519p3/8361.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/8361.Dorothy_Dunnett">Dorothy Dunnett
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
676
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer562825467218897690">Dorothy Dunnett OBE was a Scottish historical novelist. She is best known for her six-part series about Francis Crawford of Lymond, <i>The Lymond Chronicles</i>, which she followed with the eight-part prequel <i>The House of Niccolò</i>. She also wrote a novel about the real Macbeth called <i>King Hereafter</i> and a series of mystery novels centered on Johnson Johnson, a portrait painter/spy.<br/><br/><i>Her New York times obitua</i></span>
  <span id="freeText562825467218897690" style="display:none">Dorothy Dunnett OBE was a Scottish historical novelist. She is best known for her six-part series about Francis Crawford of Lymond, <i>The Lymond Chronicles</i>, which she followed with the eight-part prequel <i>The House of Niccolò</i>. She also wrote a novel about the real Macbeth called <i>King Hereafter</i> and a series of mystery novels centered on Johnson Johnson, a portrait painter/spy.<br /><br /><i>Her New York times obituary is <a href="http://query.nytimes.com/gst/fullpage.html?res=9B04E5DE1038F930A25752C1A9679C8B63" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.</i><br /><br />Dorothy Dunnett Society: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://dorothydunnett.org">http://dorothydunnett.org</a><br />Fansite: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.dorothydunnett.co.uk/">http://www.dorothydunnett.co.uk/</a></span>
